<p>The <b>Arctic Ocean</b> is the smallest and shallowest of the world's five major <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup> It spans an area of approximately 14,060,000 km<sup>2</sup> (5,430,000 sq mi) and is known as the coldest of all the oceans. The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> (IHO) recognizes it as an ocean, although some <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanographers</a> call it the <b>Arctic Mediterranean Sea</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It has been described approximately as an <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is also seen as the northernmost part of the all-encompassing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>.
</p><p>The Arctic Ocean includes the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> region in the middle of the Northern Hemisphere and extends south to about 60°N. The Arctic Ocean is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, and the borders follow topographic features: the Bering Strait on the Pacific side and the Greenland Scotland Ridge on the Atlantic side. It is mostly covered by <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> throughout the year and almost completely in <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>. The Arctic Ocean's <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">surface temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> vary seasonally as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">ice cover</a> melts and freezes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> its salinity is the lowest on average of the five major oceans, due to low <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, heavy <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> inflow from rivers and streams, and limited connection and outflow to surrounding oceanic waters with higher salinities. The summer shrinking of the ice has been quoted at 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/National_Snow_and_Ice_Data_Center" title="National Snow and Ice Data Center">National Snow and Ice Data Center</a> (NSIDC) uses satellite data to provide a daily record of Arctic sea ice cover and the rate of melting compared to an average period and specific past years, showing a continuous <a href="/wiki/Arctic_sea_ice_decline" title="Arctic sea ice decline">decline in sea ice</a> extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In September 2012, the Arctic ice extent reached a new record minimum. Compared to the average extent (1979–2000), the sea ice had diminished by 49%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif/220px-Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif/330px-Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decrease_of_old_Arctic_Sea_ice_1982-2007.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Decrease of old Arctic Sea ice 1982–2007</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In Canada, Inuit Country is called <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Nunangat" title="Inuit Nunangat">Inuit Nunangat</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a> name for the ocean is ᐅᑭᐅᖅᑕᖅᑑᑉ ᐃᒪᖓ <i>Ukiuqtaqtuup Imanga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In other languages of the Arctic region it is called:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>: <i lang="fi">Pohjoinen jäämeri</i> ("Northern Icy Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Océan Arctique, Océan Glacial arctique</i> ("Arctic [Glacial] Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>: <i lang="is">Norður-Íshaf</i> ("Northern Ice Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livvi_language" title="Livvi language">Livvi</a>: <i lang="olo">Pohjaine jiämeri</i> ("Northern Icy Ocean")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>: <i lang="no">Nordishavet, Polhavet</i> ("North Ocean", "Pole Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Северный Ледовитый океан</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">Sévernyy Ledovítyy okeán</i></span> ("Northern Ice-Covered Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>: <i lang="sv">Norra ishavet, Arktiska havet, Arktiska oceanen</i> ("Northern Ice-sea, Arctic Sea, Arctic Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Human habitation in the North American polar region goes back at least 17,000–50,000 years, during the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_glaciation" title="Wisconsin glaciation">Wisconsin glaciation</a>. At this time, falling sea levels allowed people to move across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bering_land_bridge" title="Bering land bridge">Bering land bridge</a> that joined <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> to northwestern North America (Alaska), leading to the <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goebel_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goebel-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3468" data-file-width="5304" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg/220px-Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg/330px-Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg/440px-Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cambridge_Bay_Thule_Site_1998-06-28.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thule archaeological site</div></div></div>
<p>Early Paleo-Eskimo groups included the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Dorset" title="Pre-Dorset">Pre-Dorset</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3200–850 BC</span>); the <a href="/wiki/Saqqaq_culture" title="Saqqaq culture">Saqqaq culture</a> of Greenland (2500–800 BC); the <a href="/wiki/Independence_I_culture" title="Independence I culture">Independence I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Independence_II_culture" title="Independence II culture">Independence II cultures</a> of northeastern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2400–1800 BC</span> and <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 800–1 BC</span>); and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Groswater&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Groswater (page does not exist)">Groswater</a> of <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nunavik" title="Nunavik">Nunavik</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dorset_culture" title="Dorset culture">Dorset culture</a> spread across Arctic North America between 500 BC and AD 1500. The Dorset were the last major Paleo-Eskimo culture in the Arctic before the migration east from present-day <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Thule_people" title="Thule people">Thule</a>, the ancestors of the modern Inuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thule_Tradition" title="Thule Tradition">Thule Tradition</a> lasted from about 200 BC to AD 1600, arising around the Bering Strait and later encompassing almost the entire Arctic region of North America. The Thule people were the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, who now live in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavik" title="Nunavik">northern Quebec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waterloo_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-waterloo-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For much of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">European history</a>, the north <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar regions</a> remained largely unexplored and their geography conjectural. <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massilia</a> recorded an account of a journey northward in 325 BC, to a land he called "<a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Eschate Thule</a>", where the Sun only set for three hours each day and the water was replaced by a congealed substance "on which one can neither walk nor sail". He was probably describing loose sea ice known today as "<a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">growlers</a>" or "bergy bits"; his "Thule" was probably <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, though the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> or <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> have also been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic,_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9392" data-file-width="10928" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/220px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/330px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg/440px-Map_of_the_Arctic%2C_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic,_1780s_-_B%26W.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Bowen" title="Emanuel Bowen">Emanuel Bowen</a>'s 1780s map of the Arctic features a "Northern Ocean".</div></div></div>
<p>Early <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographers</a> were unsure whether to draw the region around the North Pole as land (as in <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Ruysch" title="Johannes Ruysch">Johannes Ruysch</a>'s map of 1507, or <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a>'s map of 1595) or water (as with <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a>'s world map of 1507). The fervent desire of European merchants for a northern passage, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sea_Route" title="Northern Sea Route">Northern Sea Route</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>, to "<a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>" (<a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>) caused water to win out, and by 1723 mapmakers such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Homann" title="Johann Homann">Johann Homann</a> featured an extensive "Oceanus Septentrionalis" at the northern edge of their charts.
</p><p>The few expeditions to penetrate much beyond the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> in that era added only small islands, such as <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya" title="Novaya Zemlya">Novaya Zemlya</a> (11th century) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitzbergen" title="Spitzbergen">Spitzbergen</a> (1596), though, since these were often surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">pack-ice</a>, their northern limits were not so clear. The makers of <a href="/wiki/Nautical_chart" title="Nautical chart">navigational charts</a>, more conservative than some of the more fanciful cartographers, tended to leave the region blank, with only fragments of known coastline sketched in.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage,_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage,_and_bathymetry.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1003" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/280px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/420px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/560px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage,_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage,_and_bathymetry.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Arctic region showing the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Passage" title="Northeast Passage">Northeast Passage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sea_Route" title="Northern Sea Route">Northern Sea Route</a> within it, and the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_Century">19th Century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 19th Century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This lack of knowledge of what lay north of the shifting barrier of ice gave rise to a number of conjectures. In England and other European nations, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">myth</a> of an "<a href="/wiki/Open_Polar_Sea" title="Open Polar Sea">Open Polar Sea</a>" was persistent. <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Barrow,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir John Barrow, 1st Baronet">John Barrow</a>, longtime Second Secretary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Admiralty" title="British Admiralty">British Admiralty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_exploration" title="Arctic exploration">promoted exploration</a> of the region from 1818 to 1845 in search of this.
</p><p>In the United States in the 1850s and 1860s, the explorers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elisha_Kane" title="Elisha Kane">Elisha Kane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Israel_Hayes" title="Isaac Israel Hayes">Isaac Israel Hayes</a> both claimed to have seen part of this elusive body of water. Even quite late in the century, the eminent authority <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a> included a description of the Open Polar Sea in his textbook <i>The Physical Geography of the Sea</i> (1883). Nevertheless, as all the explorers who travelled closer and closer to the pole reported, the <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice cap</a> is quite thick and persists year-round.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">Fridtjof Nansen</a> was the first to make a <a href="/wiki/Seamanship" title="Seamanship">nautical</a> crossing of the Arctic Ocean, in 1896.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_Century">20th Century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 20th Century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first surface crossing of the ocean was led by <a href="/wiki/Wally_Herbert" title="Wally Herbert">Wally Herbert</a> in 1969, in a <a href="/wiki/Dog_sled" title="Dog sled">dog sled</a> expedition from <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> to <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, with air support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The first nautical transit of the north pole was made in 1958 by the submarine <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS <i>Nautilus</i></a>, and the first surface nautical transit occurred in 1977 by the <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreaker</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arktika_(1972_nuclear_icebreaker)" title="Arktika (1972 nuclear icebreaker)">NS <i>Arktika</i></a>.
</p><p>Since 1937, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet" title="Soviet">Soviet</a> and Russian manned <a href="/wiki/Drifting_ice_station" title="Drifting ice station">drifting ice stations</a> have extensively monitored the Arctic Ocean. Scientific settlements were established on the drift ice and carried thousands of kilometres by ice floes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the European region of the Arctic Ocean was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean_operations_of_World_War_II" title="Arctic Ocean operations of World War II">heavily contested</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_convoys" title="Arctic convoys">Allied commitment to resupply the Soviet Union via its northern ports</a> was opposed by German naval and air forces.
</p><p>Since 1954 commercial airlines have flown over the Arctic Ocean (see <a href="/wiki/Polar_route" title="Polar route">Polar route</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IBCAO_betamap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="868" data-file-width="762" decoding="async" height="376" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg/330px-IBCAO_betamap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg/495px-IBCAO_betamap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/IBCAO_betamap.jpg/660px-IBCAO_betamap.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IBCAO_betamap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetric</a>/<a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topographic</a> map of the Arctic Ocean and the surrounding lands.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="505" decoding="async" height="414" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/330px-Arctic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/495px-Arctic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Arctic.svg/660px-Arctic.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> region; of note, the region's southerly border on this map is depicted by a red <a href="/wiki/Contour_line#Temperature_and_related_subjects" title="Contour line">isotherm</a>, with all territory to the north having an average temperature of less than 10 °C (50 °F) in July.</div></div></div>
<p>The Arctic Ocean occupies a roughly circular basin and covers an area of about 14,056,000 km<sup>2</sup> (5,427,000 sq mi), almost the size of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oceans_of_the_World_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oceans_of_the_World-22">[22]</a></sup> The coastline is 45,390 km (28,200 mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arctic_Ocean_Fast_Facts_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arctic_Ocean_Fast_Facts-23">[23]</a></sup> It is the only <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> smaller than <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, which has a land area of 16,377,742 km<sup>2</sup> (6,323,482 sq mi). It is surrounded by the land masses of Eurasia, North America (including <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>), and Iceland. It is generally taken to include <a href="/wiki/Baffin_Bay" title="Baffin Bay">Baffin Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Sea" title="Beaufort Sea">Beaufort Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Siberian_Sea" title="East Siberian Sea">East Siberian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland_Sea" title="Iceland Sea">Iceland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Strait" title="Hudson Strait">Hudson Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kara_Sea" title="Kara Sea">Kara Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laptev_Sea" title="Laptev Sea">Laptev Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> and other tributary bodies of water. It is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> and to the Atlantic Ocean through the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pidwirny_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pidwirny-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Countries bordering the Arctic Ocean are <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, Greenland (territory of the Kingdom of Denmark), Canada, and the United States.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_and_major_ports">Extent and major ports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Extent and major ports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Arctic_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">Arctic Ocean</a></div>
<p>There are several ports and <a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">harbours</a> on the Arctic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Alaska, the main ports are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utqia%C4%A1vik" title="Utqiaġvik">Utqiaġvik</a> (Barrow) (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=71_17_44_N_156_45_59_W_type:city_region:US&amp;title=Barrow"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">71°17′44″N</span> <span class="longitude">156°45′59″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">71.29556°N 156.76639°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">71.29556; -156.76639</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Barrow</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Prudhoe_Bay,_Alaska" title="Prudhoe Bay, Alaska">Prudhoe Bay</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=70_19_32_N_148_42_41_W_type:city_region:US&amp;title=Prudhoe"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">70°19′32″N</span> <span class="longitude">148°42′41″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">70.32556°N 148.71139°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">70.32556; -148.71139</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Prudhoe</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, ships may anchor at <a href="/wiki/Churchill,_Manitoba" title="Churchill, Manitoba">Churchill</a> (<a href="/wiki/Port_of_Churchill" title="Port of Churchill">Port of Churchill</a>) (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=58_46_28_N_94_11_37_W_type:city_region:CA-MB&amp;title=Port+of+Churchill"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">58°46′28″N</span> <span class="longitude">94°11′37″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">58.77444°N 94.19361°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">58.77444; -94.19361</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Port of Churchill</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanisivik" title="Nanisivik">Nanisivik</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nanisivik_Naval_Facility" title="Nanisivik Naval Facility">Nanisivik Naval Facility</a>) (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=73_04_08_N_84_32_57_W_type:city_region:CA-MB&amp;title=Nanisivik+Naval+Facility"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">73°04′08″N</span> <span class="longitude">84°32′57″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">73.06889°N 84.54917°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">73.06889; -84.54917</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Nanisivik Naval Facility</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tuktoyaktuk" title="Tuktoyaktuk">Tuktoyaktuk</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=69_26_34_N_133_01_52_W_type:city_region:CA-NT&amp;title=Tuktoyaktuk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°26′34″N</span> <span class="longitude">133°01′52″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.44278°N 133.03111°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">69.44278; -133.03111</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Tuktoyaktuk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Inuvik" title="Inuvik">Inuvik</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=68_21_42_N_133_43_50_W_type:city_region:CA-NT&amp;title=Inuvik"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">68°21′42″N</span> <span class="longitude">133°43′50″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">68.36167°N 133.73056°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">68.36167; </p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland">Greenland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Greenland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Greenland, the main port is at <a href="/wiki/Nuuk" title="Nuuk">Nuuk</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nuuk_Port_and_Harbour" title="Nuuk Port and Harbour">Nuuk Port and Harbour</a>) (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=64_10_15_N_51_43_15_W_type:city_region:GL&amp;title=Nuuk+Port+and+Harbour"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">64°10′15″N</span> <span class="longitude">51°43′15″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">64.17083°N 51.72083°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">64.17083; -51.72083</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Nuuk Port and Harbour</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirkenes" title="Kirkenes">Kirkenes</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=69_43_37_N_30_02_44_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Kirkenes"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">69°43′37″N</span> <span class="longitude">30°02′44″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">69.72694°N 30.04556°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">69.72694; 30.04556</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Kirkenes</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Vard%C3%B8" title="Vardø">Vardø</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=70_22_14_N_31_06_27_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Vard%C3%B8"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">70°22′14″N</span> <span class="longitude">31°06′27″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">70.37056°N 31.10750°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">70.37056; 31.10750</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Vardø</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) are ports on the mainland. Also, there is <a href="/wiki/Longyearbyen" title="Longyearbyen">Longyearbyen</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=78_13_12_N_15_39_00_E_region:NO_type:city&amp;title=Longyearbyen"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">78°13′12″N</span> <span class="longitude">15°39′00″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">78.22000°N 15.65000°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">78.22000; 15.65000</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Longyearbyen</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) on <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, a Norwegian archipelago, next to <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Russia, major ports sorted by the different sea areas are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Murmansk" title="Murmansk">Murmansk</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=68_58_N_33_05_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Murmansk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">68°58′N</span> <span class="longitude">33°05′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">68.967°N 33.083°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">68.967; 33.083</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Murmansk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the Barents Sea;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Arkhangelsk</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=64_32_N_40_32_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Arkhangelsk"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">64°32′N</span> <span class="longitude">40°32′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">64.533°N 40.533°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">64.533; 40.533</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Arkhangelsk</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) in the White Sea;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Labytnangi" title="Labytnangi">Labytnangi</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Arctic_Ocean&amp;params=66_39_26_N_66_25_06_E_type:city_region:RU&amp;title=Labytnangi"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">66°39′26″N</span> <span class="longitude">66°25′06″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">66.65722°N 66.41833°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">66.65722; <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_shelves"><span class="anchor" id="Arctic_shelf"></span>Arctic shelves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Arctic shelves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ocean's Arctic shelf comprises a number of <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>, including the Canadian Arctic shelf, underlying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic_Archipelago" title="Canadian Arctic Archipelago">Canadian Arctic Archipelago</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf_of_Russia" title="Continental shelf of Russia">Russian continental shelf</a>, which is sometimes simply called the "Arctic Shelf" because it is greater in extent. The Russian continental shelf consists of three separate, smaller shelves: the Barents Shelf, <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea_Shelf" title="Chukchi Sea Shelf">Chukchi Sea Shelf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Shelf" title="Siberian Shelf">Siberian Shelf</a>. Of these three, the Siberian Shelf is the largest such shelf in the world; it holds large oil and gas reserves. The Chukchi shelf forms the border between Russian and the United States as stated in the <a href="/wiki/USSR%E2%80%93USA_Maritime_Boundary_Agreement" title="USSR–USA Maritime Boundary Agreement">USSR–USA Maritime Boundary Agreement</a>. The whole area is subject to international <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">territorial claims</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underwater_features">Underwater features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Underwater features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">underwater ridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_Ridge" title="Lomonosov Ridge">Lomonosov Ridge</a>, divides the deep sea <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">North Polar Basin</a> into two <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">oceanic basins</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Basin" title="Eurasian Basin">Eurasian Basin</a>, which is 4,000–4,500 m (13,100–14,800 ft) deep, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerasian_Basin" title="Amerasian Basin">Amerasian Basin</a> (sometimes called the North American or Hyperborean Basin), which is about 4,000 m (13,000 ft) deep. The <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> of the ocean bottom is marked by <a href="/wiki/Fault_block" title="Fault block">fault block</a> ridges, <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">ocean deeps</a>, and basins. The average depth of the Arctic Ocean is 1,038 m (3,406 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The deepest point is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molloy_Hole" title="Molloy Hole">Molloy Hole</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>, at about 5,550 m (18,210 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The two major basins are further subdivided by ridges into the <a href="/wiki/Canada_Basin" title="Canada Basin">Canada Basin</a> (between Beaufort Shelf of North America and the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Ridge" title="Alpha Ridge">Alpha Ridge</a>), <a href="/wiki/Makarov_Basin" title="Makarov Basin">Makarov Basin</a> (between the Alpha and Lomonosov Ridges), <a href="/wiki/Amundsen_Basin" title="Amundsen Basin">Amundsen Basin</a> (between Lomonosov and <a href="/wiki/Gakkel_Ridge" title="Gakkel Ridge">Gakkel</a> ridges), and <a href="/wiki/Nansen_Basin" title="Nansen Basin">Nansen Basin</a> (between the Gakkel Ridge and the continental shelf that includes the <a href="/wiki/Franz_Josef_Land" title="Franz Josef Land">Franz Josef Land</a>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biggest_seas_in_the_Arctic_Ocean">Biggest seas in the Arctic Ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biggest seas in the Arctic Ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The largest seas in the Arctic Ocean:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_exploration_in_the_Arctic" title="Petroleum exploration in the Arctic">Petroleum exploration in the Arctic</a></div>
<p>The crystalline basement rocks of mountains around the Arctic Ocean were recrystallized or formed during the Ellesmerian orogeny, the regional phase of the larger <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_orogeny" title="Caledonian orogeny">Caledonian orogeny</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> Era. Regional subsidence in the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> periods led to significant sediment deposition, creating many of the reservoirs for current day oil and gas deposits. During the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period, the Canadian Basin opened, and tectonic activity due to the assembly of Alaska caused hydrocarbons to migrate toward what is now Prudhoe Bay. At the same time, sediments shed off the rising Canadian Rockies built out the large Mackenzie Delta.
</p><p>The rifting apart of the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pangea" title="Pangea">Pangea</a>, beginning in the Triassic period, opened the early Atlantic Ocean. Rifting then extended northward, opening the Arctic Ocean as <a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">mafic</a> oceanic crust material erupted out of a branch of Mid-Atlantic Ridge. The Amerasia Basin may have opened first, with the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Plateau" title="Chukchi Plateau">Chukchi Borderland</a> moved along to the northeast by transform faults. Additional spreading helped to create the "triple-junction" of the Alpha-Mendeleev Ridge in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">Late Cretaceous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epoch</a>.
</p><p>Throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> Era, the subduction of the Pacific plate, the collision of India with Eurasia, and the continued opening of the North Atlantic created new hydrocarbon traps. The seafloor began spreading from the Gakkel Ridge in the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> Epoch and the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> Epoch, causing the Lomonosov Ridge to move farther from land and subside.
</p><p>Because of sea ice and remote conditions, the geology of the Arctic Ocean is still poorly explored. The Arctic Coring Expedition drilling shed some light on the Lomonosov Ridge, which appears to be continental crust separated from the Barents-Kara Shelf in the Paleocene and then starved of sediment. It may contain up to 10 billion barrels of oil. The Gakkel Ridge rift is also poorly understand and may extend into the Laptev Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanography">Oceanography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Oceanography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_flow">Water flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Water flow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:472px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg/470px-BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg/705px-BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg/940px-BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg.png 2x" width="470"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BrnBld_BeringToFram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of the major <a href="/wiki/Water_mass" title="Water mass">water mass</a> in the Arctic Ocean. The section sketches the different water masses along a vertical section from <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> over the geographic <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> to <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Stratification_(water)" title="Stratification (water)">stratification</a> is stable, deeper water masses are denser than the layers above.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="271" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/330px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/495px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg/660px-Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_and_salinity_profiles_in_the_Arctic_Ocean.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Density structure of the upper 1,200 m (3,900 ft) in the Arctic Ocean. Profiles of temperature and salinity for the Amundsen Basin, the Canadian Basin and the Greenland Sea are sketched.</div></div></div>
<p>In large parts of the Arctic Ocean, the top layer (about 50 m [160 ft]) is of lower salinity and lower temperature than the rest. It remains relatively stable because the salinity effect on density is bigger than the temperature effect. It is fed by the freshwater input of the big Siberian and Canadian rivers (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ob_River" title="Ob River">Ob</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lena_River" title="Lena River">Lena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackenzie_River" title="Mackenzie River">Mackenzie</a>), the water of which quasi floats on the saltier, denser, deeper ocean water. Between this lower salinity layer and the bulk of the ocean lies the so-called <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>, in which both salinity and temperature rise with increasing depth.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copepodkils.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/220px-Copepodkils.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/330px-Copepodkils.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Copepodkils.jpg/440px-Copepodkils.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copepodkils.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepod</a></div></div></div>
<p>Because of its relative isolation from other oceans, the Arctic Ocean has a uniquely complex system of water flow. It resembles some hydrological features of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, referring to its deep waters having only limited communication through the <a href="/wiki/Fram_Strait" title="Fram Strait">Fram Strait</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Basin" title="Atlantic Basin">Atlantic Basin</a>, "where the circulation is dominated by thermohaline forcing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> The Arctic Ocean has a total volume of 18.07 × 10<sup>6</sup> km<sup>3</sup>, equal to about 1.3% of the World Ocean.  Mean surface circulation is predominantly cyclonic on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian" title="Eurasian">Eurasian</a> side and anticyclonic in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Basin" title="Canadian Basin">Canadian Basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water enters from both the Pacific and Atlantic Oceans and can be divided into three unique water masses. The deepest water mass is called Arctic Bottom Water and begins around 900 m (3,000 ft) depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> It is composed of the densest water in the World Ocean and has two main sources: Arctic shelf water and Greenland Sea Deep Water. Water in the shelf region that begins as inflow from the Pacific passes through the narrow <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> at an average rate of 0.8 <a href="/wiki/Sverdrup" title="Sverdrup">Sverdrups</a> and reaches the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Sea" title="Chukchi Sea">Chukchi Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-36">[36]</a></sup> During the winter, cold Alaskan winds blow over the Chukchi Sea, freezing the surface water and pushing this newly formed ice out to the Pacific. The speed of the ice drift is roughly 1–4 cm/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-35">[35]</a></sup> This process leaves dense, salty waters in the sea that sink over the continental shelf into the western Arctic Ocean and create a <a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nares_strait_border_(Kennedy_channel).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1249" data-file-width="1765" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/220px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/330px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png/440px-Nares_strait_border_%28Kennedy_channel%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nares_strait_border_(Kennedy_channel).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Channel" title="Kennedy Channel">Kennedy Channel</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>This water is met by Greenland Sea Deep Water, which forms during the passage of winter storms. As temperatures cool dramatically in the winter, ice forms, and intense vertical convection allows the water to become dense enough to sink below the warm saline water below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> Arctic Bottom Water is critically important because of its outflow, which contributes to the formation of Atlantic Deep Water. The overturning of this water plays a key role in global circulation and the moderation of climate.
</p><p>In the depth range of 150–900 m (490–2,950 ft) is a water mass referred to as Atlantic Water. Inflow from the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> enters through the Fram Strait, cooling and sinking to form the deepest layer of the halocline, where it circles the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">Arctic Basin</a> counter-clockwise. This is the highest volumetric inflow to the Arctic Ocean, equalling about 10 times that of the Pacific inflow, and it creates the Arctic Ocean Boundary Current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-36">[36]</a></sup> It flows slowly, at about 0.02 m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> Atlantic Water has the same salinity as Arctic Bottom Water but is much warmer (up to 3 °C [37 °F]). In fact, this water mass is actually warmer than the surface water and remains submerged only due to the role of salinity in density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> When water reaches the basin, it is pushed by strong winds into a large circular current called the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_Gyre" title="Beaufort Gyre">Beaufort Gyre</a>. Water in the Beaufort Gyre is far less saline than that of the Chukchi Sea due to inflow from large Canadian and Siberian rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polar-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final defined water mass in the Arctic Ocean is called Arctic Surface Water and is found in the depth range of 150–200 m (490–660 ft). The most important feature of this water mass is a section referred to as the sub-surface layer. It is a product of Atlantic water that enters through canyons and is subjected to intense mixing on the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Shelf" title="Siberian Shelf">Siberian Shelf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> As it is entrained, it cools and acts a heat shield for the surface layer on account of weak mixing between layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, over the past couple of decades a combination of the warming<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and the shoaling of Atlantic water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> are leading to the increasing influence of Atlantic water heat in melting sea ice in the eastern Arctic. The most recent estimates, for 2016–2018, indicate the oceanic heat flux to the surface has now overtaken the atmospheric flux in the eastern Eurasian Basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Over the same period the weakening halocline stratification has coincided with increasing upper ocean currents thought to be associated with declining sea ice, indicate increasing mixing in this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In contrast direct measurements of mixing in the western Arctic indicate the Atlantic water heat remains isolated at intermediate depths even under the 'perfect storm' conditions of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Arctic_Cyclone_of_2012" title="Great Arctic Cyclone of 2012">Great Arctic Cyclone of 2012</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Waters originating in the Pacific and Atlantic both exit through the Fram Strait between <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Svalbard_Island" title="Svalbard Island">Svalbard Island</a>, which is about 2,700 m (8,900 ft) deep and 350 km (220 mi) wide. This outflow is about 9 Sv.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-36">[36]</a></sup> The width of the Fram Strait is what allows for both inflow and outflow on the Atlantic side of the Arctic Ocean. Because of this, it is influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a>, which concentrates outflow to the East Greenland Current on the western side and inflow to the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Current" title="Norwegian Current">Norwegian Current</a> on the eastern side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomczak_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tomczak-34">[34]</a></sup> Pacific water also exits along the west coast of Greenland and the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Strait" title="Hudson Strait">Hudson Strait</a> (1–2 Sv), providing nutrients to the Canadian Archipelago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>As noted, the process of ice formation and movement is a key driver in Arctic Ocean circulation and the formation of water masses. With this dependence, the Arctic Ocean experiences variations due to seasonal changes in sea ice cover. Sea ice movement is the result of wind forcing, which is related to a number of meteorological conditions that the Arctic experiences throughout the year. For example, the Beaufort High—an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_High" title="Siberian High">Siberian High</a> system—is a pressure system that drives the anticyclonic motion of the Beaufort Gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-35">[35]</a></sup> During the summer, this area of high pressure is pushed out closer to its Siberian and Canadian sides. In addition, there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_pressure" title="Sea level pressure">sea level pressure</a> (SLP) ridge over Greenland that drives strong northerly winds through the Fram Strait, facilitating ice export. In the summer, the SLP contrast is smaller, producing weaker winds. A final example of seasonal pressure system movement is the low pressure system that exists over the Nordic and Barents Seas. It is an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Low" title="Icelandic Low">Icelandic Low</a>, which creates cyclonic ocean circulation in this area. The low shifts to centre over the North Pole in the summer. These variations in the Arctic all contribute to ice drift reaching its weakest point during the summer months.  There is also evidence that the drift is associated with the phase of the Arctic Oscillation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Multidecadal_Oscillation" title="Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation">Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talley_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Talley-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_ice">Sea ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sea ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/220px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/330px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/440px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea cover in the Arctic Ocean, showing the median, 2005 and 2007 coverage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">Arctic ice pack</a></div>
<p>Much of the Arctic Ocean is covered by sea ice that varies in extent and thickness seasonally. The mean extent of the Arctic sea ice has been continuously decreasing in the last decades, declining at a rate of currently 12.85% per decade since 1980 from the average winter value of 15,600,000 km<sup>2</sup> (6,023,200 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The seasonal variations are about 7,000,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,702,700 sq mi), with the maximum in April and minimum in September. The sea ice is affected by wind and ocean currents, which can move and rotate very large areas of ice. Zones of compression also arise, where the ice piles up to form pack ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">Icebergs</a> occasionally break away from northern <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Island" title="Ellesmere Island">Ellesmere Island</a>, and icebergs are formed from <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> in western Greenland and extreme northeastern Canada. Icebergs are not sea ice but may become embedded in the pack ice. Icebergs pose a hazard to ships, of which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic" title="RMS Titanic"><i>Titanic</i></a> is one of the most famous. The ocean is virtually icelocked from October to June, and the <a href="/wiki/Superstructure" title="Superstructure">superstructure</a> of ships are subject to <a href="/wiki/Icing_(nautical)" title="Icing (nautical)">icing</a> from October to May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-24">[24]</a></sup> Before the advent of modern <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>, ships sailing the Arctic Ocean risked being trapped or crushed by sea ice (although the <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Baychimo" title="SS Baychimo">Baychimo</a></i> drifted through the Arctic Ocean untended for decades despite these hazards).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">Climate change in the Arctic</a></div>
